talk:Linguistics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Linguistics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Linguistics</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> study of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is called a scientific study because it entails a comprehensive, systematic, objective, and precise analysis of all aspects of language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> particularly its nature and structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Linguistics is concerned with both the <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> and social aspects of language.  It is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_field" title="Scientific field">scientific field</a> as well as an <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linguisticsociety.org_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-linguisticsociety.org-5">[5]</a></sup> it has been classified as a <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford1-8">[8]</a></sup> or part of the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>.
</p><p>Traditional areas of linguistic analysis correspond to phenomena found in human linguistic systems, such as <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> (rules governing the structure of sentences); <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a> (meaning); <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> (structure of words); <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetics</a> (speech sounds and equivalent gestures in <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign languages</a>); <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> (the abstract sound system of a particular language); and <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">pragmatics</a> (how social context contributes to meaning).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Subdisciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Biolinguistics" title="Biolinguistics">biolinguistics</a> (the study of the biological variables and evolution of language) and <a href="/wiki/Psycholinguistics" title="Psycholinguistics">psycholinguistics</a> (the study of psychological factors in human language) bridge many of these divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguistics encompasses <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_linguistics" title="Outline of linguistics">many branches and subfields</a> that span both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theoretical" title="Theoretical">theoretical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Practical" title="Practical">practical</a> applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-linguisticsociety.org_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-linguisticsociety.org-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_linguistics" title="Theoretical linguistics">Theoretical linguistics</a> (including traditional descriptive linguistics) is concerned with understanding the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language#Nature_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">fundamental nature of language</a> and developing a general theoretical framework for describing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Applied_linguistics" title="Applied linguistics">Applied linguistics</a> seeks to utilise the scientific findings of the study of language for practical purposes, such as developing methods of improving <a href="/wiki/Language_education" title="Language education">language education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguistic phenomena may be studied through a variety of perspectives: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronic_analysis" title="Synchronic analysis">synchronically</a> (describing a language at a specific point of time) or <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">diachronically</a> (through historical development); in <a href="/wiki/Monolingualism" title="Monolingualism">monolinguals</a> or <a href="/wiki/Multilingualism" title="Multilingualism">multilinguals</a>; children or adults; as they are learned or already acquired; as abstract objects or cognitive structures; through texts or oral elicitation; and through mechanical data collection versus fieldwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Linguistics is related to <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stylistics" title="Stylistics">stylistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetorics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">lexicography</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">translation</a>; <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>, from which linguistics emerged, is variably described as a related field, a subdiscipline, or to have been superseded altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_subdisciplines">Major subdisciplines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Major subdisciplines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_linguistics">Historical linguistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical linguistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">Historical linguistics</a></div>
<p>Historical linguistics is the study of how language changes in history, particularly with regard to a specific language or a group of languages. <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western trends</a> in historical linguistics date back to roughly the late 18th century, when the discipline grew out of <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>, the study of ancient texts and oral traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historical linguistics emerged as one of the first few sub-disciplines in the field, and was most widely practiced during the late 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Despite a shift in focus in the twentieth century towards <a href="/wiki/Formalism_(linguistics)" title="Formalism (linguistics)">formalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generative_grammar" title="Generative grammar">generative grammar</a>, which studies the <a href="/wiki/Universal_grammar" title="Universal grammar">universal</a> properties of language, historical research today still remains a significant field of linguistic inquiry. Subfields of the discipline include <a href="/wiki/Language_change" title="Language change">language change</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammaticalisation" title="Grammaticalisation">grammaticalisation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historical linguistics studies language change either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diachronic_linguistics" title="Diachronic linguistics">diachronically</a> (through a comparison of different time periods in the past and present) or in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronic_linguistics" title="Synchronic linguistics">synchronic</a> manner (by observing developments between different variations that exist within the current linguistic stage of a language).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Modern_Science_-_Linguistics_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Science_-_Linguistics-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>At first, historical linguistics served as the cornerstone of <a href="/wiki/Comparative_linguistics" title="Comparative linguistics">comparative linguistics</a>, which involves a study of the relationship between different languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_introduction_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge_introduction-19">[19]</a></sup> During this time, scholars of historical linguistics were only concerned with creating different categories of <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language families</a>, and reconstructing <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistoric</a> proto languages by using both the <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method" title="Comparative method">comparative method</a> and the method of <a href="/wiki/Internal_reconstruction" title="Internal reconstruction">internal reconstruction</a>. Internal reconstruction is the method by which an element that contains a certain meaning is re-used in different contexts or environments where there is a variation in either sound or analogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_introduction_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge_introduction-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The reason for this had been to describe well-known <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, many of which used to have long written histories. Scholars of historical linguistics also studied <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic languages</a>, another <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">European language</a> family for which very little written material existed back then. After this, there was significant work that followed on the corpora of other languages too, such as that of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>, as well as of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_language" title="Native American language">Native American language families</a>.
</p><p>The above approach of comparativism in linguistics is now, however, only a small part of the much broader discipline called historical linguistics. The comparative study of specific Indo-European languages is considered a highly specialised field today, while comparative research is carried out over the subsequent internal developments in a language. In particular, it is carried out over the development of modern standard varieties of languages, or over the development of a language from its standardized form to its varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Modern_Science_-_Linguistics_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Science_-_Linguistics-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>For instance, some scholars also undertook a study attempting to establish super-families, linking, for example, Indo-European, Uralic, and other language families to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nostratic" title="Nostratic">Nostratic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> While these attempts are still not widely accepted as credible methods, they provide necessary information to establish relatedness in language change, something that is not easily available as the depth of time increases. The time-depth of linguistic methods is generally limited, due to the occurrence of chance word resemblances and variations between language groups, but a limit of around 10,000 years is often assumed for the functional purpose of conducting research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Difficulty also exists in the dating of various proto languages. Even though several methods are available, only approximate results can be obtained in terms of arriving at dates for these languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, with a subsequent re-development of grammatical studies, historical linguistics studies the change in language on a relational basis between dialect to dialect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronic_linguistics" title="Synchronic linguistics">during one period</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diachronic_linguistics" title="Diachronic linguistics">between those in the past and the present period</a>, and looks at evolution and shifts taking place morphologically, syntactically, as well as phonetically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acadmia_Morphology_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acadmia_Morphology-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax_and_morphology">Syntax and morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Syntax and morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">Syntax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">Morphology (linguistics)</a></div>
<p>Syntax and morphology are branches of linguistics concerned with the order and structure of meaningful linguistic units such as words and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphemes" title="Morphemes">morphemes</a>. Syntacticians study the rules and constraints that govern how speakers of a language can organize words into sentences. Morphologists study similar rules for the order of morphemes—sub-word units such as prefixes and suffixes—and how they may be combined to form words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acadmia_Morphology_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Acadmia_Morphology-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>While words, along with <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitics</a>, are generally accepted as being the smallest units of <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a>, in most languages, if not all, many words can be related to other words by rules that collectively describe the <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> for that language. For example, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> speakers recognize that the words <i>dog</i> and <i>dogs</i> are closely related, differentiated only by the <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plurality</a> <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> "-s", only found <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bound_morpheme" title="Bound morpheme">bound</a> to <a href="/wiki/Noun_phrase" title="Noun phrase">noun phrases</a>. Speakers of English recognize these relations from their innate knowledge of English's rules of <a href="/wiki/Word_formation" title="Word formation">word formation</a>. They infer intuitively that <i>dog</i> is to <i>dogs</i> as <i>cat</i> is to <i>cats</i>; and, in similar fashion, <i>dog</i> is to <i>dog catcher</i> as <i>dish</i> is to <i>dishwasher</i>. By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> has very little morphology, using almost exclusively unbound morphemes ("free" morphemes) and depending on <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> to convey meaning. (Most words in modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> ["Mandarin"], however, are <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a> and most <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a> are bound.) These are understood as grammars that represent the morphology of the language. The rules understood by a speaker reflect specific patterns or regularities in the way words are formed from smaller units in the language they are using, and how those smaller units interact in speech. In this way, morphology is the branch of linguistics that studies patterns of word formation within and across languages and attempts to formulate rules that model the knowledge of the speakers of those languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonological" title="Phonological">Phonological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthographic</a> modifications between a base word and its origin may be partial to <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a> skills. Studies have indicated that the presence of modification in phonology and orthography makes morphologically complex words harder to understand and that the absence of modification between a base word and its origin makes morphologically complex words easier to understand. Morphologically complex words are easier to comprehend when they include a base word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polysynthetic_language" title="Polysynthetic language">Polysynthetic languages</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_language" title="Chukchi language">Chukchi</a>, have words composed of many morphemes. The Chukchi word "təmeyŋəlevtpəγtərkən", for example, meaning "I have a fierce headache", is composed of eight morphemes <i>t-ə-meyŋ-ə-levt-pəγt-ə-rkən</i> that may be <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">glossed</a>. The morphology of such languages allows for each <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> to be understood as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphemes" title="Morphemes">morphemes</a>, while the grammar of the language indicates the usage and understanding of each morpheme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discipline that deals specifically with the sound changes occurring within morphemes is <a href="/wiki/Morphophonology" title="Morphophonology">morphophonology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantics_and_pragmatics">Semantics and pragmatics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Semantics and pragmatics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_semantics_(linguistics)" title="Formal semantics (linguistics)">Formal semantics (linguistics)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_semantics" title="Cognitive semantics">Cognitive semantics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">Pragmatics</a></div>
<p>Semantics and pragmatics are branches of linguistics concerned with meaning. These subfields have traditionally been divided according to aspects of meaning thought to arise from the grammar versus linguistic and social context. Semantics in this conception is concerned with grammatical and lexical meanings and pragmatics concerned with meaning in context. The framework of <a href="/wiki/Formal_semantics_(natural_language)" title="Formal semantics (natural language)">formal semantics</a> studies the <a href="/wiki/Denotation" title="Denotation">denotations</a> of sentences and the way they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compositionality" title="Compositionality">composed</a> from the meanings of their constituent expressions. Formal semantics draws heavily on <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a> and uses formal tools from <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_semantics" title="Cognitive semantics">Cognitive semantics</a> ties linguistic meaning to general aspects of cognition, drawing on ideas from <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Prototype_theory" title="Prototype theory">prototype theory</a>.
</p><p>Pragmatics encompasses phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Speech_act" title="Speech act">speech acts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Implicature" title="Implicature">implicature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Conversation_analysis" title="Conversation analysis">talk in interaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mey_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mey-28">[28]</a></sup> Unlike <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a>, which examines meaning that is conventional or "coded" in a given language, pragmatics studies how the transmission of meaning depends not only on structural and linguistic knowledge (<a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a>, etc.) of the speaker and listener but also on the context of the utterance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> any pre-existing knowledge about those involved, the inferred <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intent</a> of the speaker, and other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In that respect, pragmatics explains how language users are able to overcome apparent <a href="/wiki/Ambiguity" title="Ambiguity">ambiguity</a> since meaning relies on the manner, place, time, etc. of an utterance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mey_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mey-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetics_and_phonology">Phonetics and phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Phonetics and phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">Phonetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">Phonology</a></div>
<p>Phonetics and phonology are branches of linguistics concerned with sounds (or the equivalent aspects of sign languages). Phonetics is largely concerned with the physical aspects of sounds such as their <a href="/wiki/Articulatory_phonetics" title="Articulatory phonetics">articulation</a>, acoustics, production, and perception. Phonology is concerned with the linguistic abstractions and categorizations of sounds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typology">Typology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Typology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This paragraph is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_typology" title="Linguistic typology">Linguistic typology</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linguistic_typology&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Linguistic_typology" title="Linguistic typology">Linguistic typology</a> (or language typology) is a field of linguistics that studies and classifies languages according to their structural features to allow mass comparisons of the cross-linguistic data. Its aim is to describe and explain the structural diversity and the common properties of the world's languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Linguistic_typology_:12_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Linguistic_typology_:12-32">[32]</a></sup> Its subdisciplines include, but are not limited to phonological typology, which deals with sound features; syntactic typology, which deals with word order and form; lexical typology, which deals with language vocabulary; and theoretical typology, which aims to explain the universal tendencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_varieties">Language varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Language varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">Variety (linguistics)</a></div>
<p>Languages exist on a wide continuum of conventionalization with blurry divisions between concepts such as dialects and languages. Languages can undergo internal changes which lead to the development of subvarieties such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_register" title="Linguistic register">linguistic registers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Accent_(sociolinguistics)" title="Accent (sociolinguistics)">Accents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>. Similarly, languages can undergo changes caused by contact with speakers of other languages, and new language varieties may be born from these contact situations through the process of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Language_genesis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Language genesis (page does not exist)">language genesis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contact_varieties">Contact varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Contact varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creolistics" title="Creolistics">Creolistics</a></div>
<p>Contact varieties such as pidgins and creoles are language varieties that often arise in situations of sustained contact between communities that speak different languages. <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">Pidgins</a> are language varieties with limited conventionalization where ideas are conveyed through simplified grammars that may grow more complex as linguistic contact continues. <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">Creole languages</a> are language varieties similar to pidgins but with greater conventionalization and stability. As children grow up in contact situations, they may learn a local pidgin as their native language. Through this process of acquisition and transmission, new grammatical features and lexical items are created and introduced to fill <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexical_gap" title="Lexical gap">gaps</a> in the pidgin eventually developing into a complete language.
</p><p>Not all language contact situations result in the development of a pidgin or creole, and researchers have studied the features of contact situations that make contact varieties more likely to develop. Often these varieties arise in situations of <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslavement</a>, where power imbalances prevent the contact groups from learning the other's language but sustained contact is nevertheless maintained. The subjugated language in the power relationship is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substrate_language" title="Substrate language">substrate language</a>, while the dominant language serves as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superstrate" title="Superstrate">superstrate</a>. Often the words and lexicon of a contact variety come from the superstrate, making it the <a href="/wiki/Lexifier" title="Lexifier">lexifier</a>, while grammatical structures come from the substrate, but this is not always the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect">Dialect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dialect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A dialect is a <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">variety</a> of <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> that is characteristic of a particular group among the language's speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The group of people who are the speakers of a dialect are usually bound to each other by social identity. This is what differentiates a dialect from a <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a>, where in the latter case, cultural identity does not always play a role. Dialects are speech varieties that have their own grammatical and phonological rules, linguistic features, and stylistic aspects, but have not been given an official status as a language. Dialects often move on to gain the status of a language due to political and social reasons. Other times, dialects remain marginalized, particularly when they are associated with marginalized social groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2019)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Differentiation amongst dialects (and subsequently, languages) is based upon the use of grammatical rules, syntactic rules, and stylistic features, though not always on lexical use or vocabulary. The popular saying that "<a href="/wiki/A_language_is_a_dialect_with_an_army_and_navy" title="A language is a dialect with an army and navy">a language is a dialect with an army and navy</a>" is attributed as a definition formulated by <a href="/wiki/Max_Weinreich" title="Max Weinreich">Max Weinreich</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"We may as individuals be rather fond of our own dialect. This should not make us think, though, that it is actually any better than any other dialect. Dialects are not good or bad, nice or nasty, right or wrong – they are just different from one another, and it is the mark of a civilised society that it tolerates different dialects just as it tolerates different races, religions and sexes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_language">Standard language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Standard language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When a dialect is documented sufficiently through the linguistic description of its grammar, which has emerged through the consensual laws from within its community, it gains political and national recognition through a country or region's policies. That is the stage when a language is considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_variety" title="Standard variety">standard variety</a>, one whose grammatical laws have now stabilised from within the consent of <a href="/wiki/Speech_community" title="Speech community">speech community</a> participants, after sufficient evolution, improvisation, correction, and growth. The English language, besides perhaps the French language, may be examples of languages that have arrived at a stage where they are said to have become standard varieties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relativity">Relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As constructed popularly through the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_relativity" title="Linguistic relativity">Sapir–Whorf hypothesis</a>, relativists believe that the structure of a particular language is capable of influencing the cognitive patterns through which a person shapes his or her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a>. Universalists believe that there are commonalities between human perception as there is in the human capacity for language, while relativists believe that this varies from language to language and person to person. While the Sapir–Whorf hypothesis is an elaboration of this idea expressed through the writings of American linguists <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sapir" title="Edward Sapir">Edward Sapir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Lee_Whorf" title="Benjamin Lee Whorf">Benjamin Lee Whorf</a>, it was Sapir's student <a href="/wiki/Harry_Hoijer" title="Harry Hoijer">Harry Hoijer</a> who termed it thus. The 20th century German linguist <a href="/wiki/Leo_Weisgerber" title="Leo Weisgerber">Leo Weisgerber</a> also wrote extensively about the theory of relativity. Relativists argue for the case of differentiation at the level of cognition and in semantic domains. The emergence of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_linguistics" title="Cognitive linguistics">cognitive linguistics</a> in the 1980s also revived an interest in linguistic relativity. Thinkers like <a href="/wiki/George_Lakoff" title="George Lakoff">George Lakoff</a> have argued that language reflects different cultural metaphors, while the French philosopher of language <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">Jacques Derrida</a>'s writings, especially about <a href="/wiki/Deconstructivism" title="Deconstructivism">deconstruction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> have been seen to be closely associated with the relativist movement in linguistics, for which he was heavily criticized in the media at the time of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structures">Structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2019</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Linguistic structures are pairings of meaning and form. Any particular pairing of meaning and form is a <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Saussure" title="Ferdinand de Saussure">Saussurean</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_sign" title="Linguistic sign">sign</a>.  For instance, the meaning "cat" is represented worldwide with a wide variety of different sound patterns (in oral languages), movements of the hands and face (in <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">sign languages</a>), and written symbols (in written languages). Linguistic patterns have proven their importance for the <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_engineering" title="Knowledge engineering">knowledge engineering</a> field especially with the ever-increasing amount of available data.
</p><p>Linguists focusing on structure attempt to understand the rules regarding language use that native speakers know (not always consciously).  All linguistic structures can be broken down into component parts that are combined according to (sub)conscious rules, over multiple levels of analysis.  For instance, consider the structure of the word "tenth" on two different levels of analysis.  On the level of internal word structure (known as morphology), the word "tenth" is made up of one linguistic form indicating a number and another form indicating ordinality.  The rule governing the combination of these forms ensures that the ordinality marker "th" follows the number "ten."  On the level of sound structure (known as phonology), structural analysis shows that the "n" sound in "tenth" is made differently from the "n" sound in "ten" spoken alone.  Although most speakers of English are consciously aware of the rules governing internal structure of the word pieces of "tenth", they are less often aware of the rule governing its sound structure.  Linguists focused on structure find and analyze rules such as these, which govern how native speakers use language.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">Grammar</a> is a system of rules which governs the production and use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utterances" title="Utterances">utterances</a> in a given language. These rules apply to sound<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> as well as meaning, and include componential subsets of rules, such as those pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a> (the organisation of phonetic sound systems), <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> (the formation and composition of words), and <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> (the formation and composition of phrases and sentences).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Modern <a href="/wiki/Grammar#Theoretical_frameworks" title="Grammar">frameworks that deal with the principles of grammar</a> include <a href="/wiki/Structural_linguistics" title="Structural linguistics">structural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Functional_linguistics" title="Functional linguistics">functional linguistics</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generative_linguistics" title="Generative linguistics">generative linguistics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sub-fields that focus on a grammatical study of language include the following:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">Phonetics</a></b>, the study of the physical properties of speech sound production and perception, and delves into their acoustic and <a href="/wiki/Articulatory_phonetics" title="Articulatory phonetics">articulatory</a> properties</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">Phonology</a></b>, the study of sounds as abstract elements in the speaker's mind that distinguish meaning (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemes" title="Phonemes">phonemes</a>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">Morphology</a></b>, the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morphemes" title="Morphemes">morphemes</a>, or the internal structures of words and how they can be modified</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">Syntax</a></b>, the study of how words combine to form grammatical phrases and <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(linguistics)" title="Sentence (linguistics)">sentences</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">Semantics</a></b>, the study of lexical and grammatical aspects of meaning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meaning_and_Grammar:_An_Introductio_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meaning_and_Grammar:_An_Introductio-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">Pragmatics</a></b>, the study of how <a href="/wiki/Utterance" title="Utterance">utterances</a> are used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Speech_acts" title="Speech acts">communicative acts</a>, and the role played by situational context and non-linguistic knowledge in the transmission of meaning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meaning_and_Grammar:_An_Introductio_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meaning_and_Grammar:_An_Introductio-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Discourse_analysis" title="Discourse analysis">Discourse analysis</a></b>, the analysis of language use in <a href="/wiki/Text_(literary_theory)" title="Text (literary theory)">texts</a> (spoken, written, or signed)</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stylistics_(field_of_study)" title="Stylistics (field of study)">Stylistics</a></b>, the study of linguistic factors (rhetoric, diction, stress) that place a discourse in context</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">Semiotics</a></b>, the study of signs and sign processes (semiosis), indication, designation, likeness, analogy, metaphor, symbolism, signification, and communication</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discourse">Discourse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Discourse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Discourse is language as social practice (Baynham, 1995) and is a multilayered concept.  As a social practice, discourse embodies different ideologies through written and spoken texts. Discourse analysis can examine or expose these ideologies.  Discourse influences genre, which is chosen in response to different situations and finally, at micro level, discourse influences language as text (spoken or written) at the phonological or lexico-grammatical level. Grammar and discourse are linked as parts of a system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>  A particular discourse becomes a language variety when it is used in this way for a particular purpose, and is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> There may be certain <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexical</a> additions (new words) that are brought into play because of the expertise of the community of people within a certain domain of specialization. Registers and discourses therefore differentiate themselves through the use of <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, and at times through the use of style too. People in the medical fraternity, for example, may use some medical terminology in their communication that is specialized to the field of medicine. This is often referred to as being part of the "medical discourse", and so on.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lexicon">Lexicon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lexicon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a> is a catalogue of words and terms that are stored in a speaker's mind. The lexicon consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Words" title="Words">words</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bound_morphemes" title="Bound morphemes">bound morphemes</a>, which are parts of words that can't stand alone, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affixes" title="Affixes">affixes</a>. In some analyses, compound words and certain classes of idiomatic expressions and other collocations are also considered to be part of the lexicon. Dictionaries represent attempts at listing, in alphabetical order, the lexicon of a given language; usually, however, bound morphemes are not included. <a href="/wiki/Lexicography" title="Lexicography">Lexicography</a>, closely linked with the domain of semantics, is the science of mapping the words into an <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedia</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a>. The creation and addition of new words (into the lexicon) is called coining or neologization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and the new words are called <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologisms</a>.
</p><p>It is often believed that a speaker's capacity for language lies in the quantity of words stored in the lexicon. However, this is often considered a myth by linguists. The capacity for the use of language is considered by many linguists to lie primarily in the domain of grammar, and to be linked with <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_competence" title="Linguistic competence">competence</a>, rather than with the growth of vocabulary. Even a very small lexicon is theoretically capable of producing an infinite number of sentences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stylistics_(field_of_study)" title="Stylistics (field of study)">Stylistics</a> also involves the study of written, signed, or spoken <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a> through varying speech communities, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genres" title="Genres">genres</a>, and editorial or <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narrative</a> formats in the mass media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> It involves the study and interpretation of texts for aspects of their linguistic and tonal style. Stylistic analysis entails the analysis of description of particular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialects" title="Dialects">dialects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a> used by speech communities. Stylistic features include <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> diction, stress, <a href="/wiki/Satire" title="Satire">satire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a>, dialogue, and other forms of phonetic variations. Stylistic analysis can also include the study of language in canonical works of literature, popular fiction, news, advertisements, and other forms of communication in popular culture as well. It is usually seen as a variation in communication that changes from speaker to speaker and community to community. In short, Stylistics is the interpretation of text.
</p><p>In the 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Derrida" title="Jacques Derrida">Jacques Derrida</a>, for instance, further distinguished between speech and writing, by proposing that written language be studied as a linguistic medium of communication in itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palaeography" title="Palaeography">Palaeography</a> is therefore the discipline that studies the evolution of written scripts (as signs and symbols) in language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The formal study of language also led to the growth of fields like <a href="/wiki/Psycholinguistics" title="Psycholinguistics">psycholinguistics</a>, which explores the representation and function of language in the mind; <a href="/wiki/Neurolinguistics" title="Neurolinguistics">neurolinguistics</a>, which studies language processing in the brain; <a href="/wiki/Biolinguistics" title="Biolinguistics">biolinguistics</a>, which studies the biology and evolution of language; and <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">language acquisition</a>, which investigates how children and adults acquire the knowledge of one or more languages.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches">Approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_language" title="Theory of language">Theory of language</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Humanistic">Humanistic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Humanistic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The fundamental principle of humanistic linguistics is that language is an invention created by people. A <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotic</a> tradition of linguistic research considers language a <a href="/wiki/Sign_system" title="Sign system">sign system</a> which arises from the interaction of meaning and form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The organisation of linguistic levels is considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computational" title="Computational">computational</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Linguistics is essentially seen as relating to <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">cultural studies</a> because different languages are shaped in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_interaction" title="Social interaction">social interaction</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Speech_community" title="Speech community">speech community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Frameworks representing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humanistic" title="Humanistic">humanistic</a> view of language include <a href="/wiki/Structural_linguistics" title="Structural linguistics">structural linguistics</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-humanistic_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-humanistic-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Structural analysis means dissecting each linguistic level: phonetic, morphological, syntactic, and discourse, to the smallest units. These are collected into inventories (e.g. phoneme, morpheme, lexical classes, phrase types) to study their interconnectedness within a hierarchy of structures and layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Functional analysis adds to structural analysis the assignment of semantic and other functional roles that each unit may have. For example, a noun phrase may function as the subject or object of the sentence; or the <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Patient_(grammar)" title="Patient (grammar)">patient</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Functional_linguistics" title="Functional linguistics">Functional linguistics</a>, or functional grammar, is a branch of structural linguistics. In the humanistic reference, the terms <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">structuralism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Structural_functionalism" title="Structural functionalism">functionalism</a> are related to their meaning in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sciences" title="Human sciences">human sciences</a>. The difference between formal and functional structuralism lies in the way that the two approaches explain why languages have the properties they have. Functional <a href="/wiki/Explanation" title="Explanation">explanation</a> entails the idea that language is a tool for <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a>, or that communication is the primary function of language. Linguistic forms are consequently explained by an appeal to their functional value, or usefulness. Other structuralist approaches take the perspective that form follows from the inner mechanisms of the bilateral and multilayered language system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological">Biological</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Biological">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Biolinguistics" title="Biolinguistics">Biolinguistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biosemiotics" title="Biosemiotics">Biosemiotics</a></div>
<p>Approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_linguistics" title="Cognitive linguistics">cognitive linguistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generative_grammar" title="Generative grammar">generative grammar</a> study linguistic <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> with a view towards uncovering the <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> underpinnings of language. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generative_Grammar" title="Generative Grammar">Generative Grammar</a>, these underpinning are understood as including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_nativism" title="Linguistic nativism">innate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domain-specific" title="Domain-specific">domain-specific</a> grammatical knowledge. Thus, one of the central concerns of the approach is to discover what aspects of linguistic knowledge are innate and which are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cognitive_Linguistics" title="Cognitive Linguistics">Cognitive Linguistics</a>, in contrast, rejects the notion of innate grammar, and studies how the human mind creates linguistic <a href="/wiki/Construction_grammar" title="Construction grammar">constructions</a> from event <a href="/wiki/Schema_(psychology)" title="Schema (psychology)">schemas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arbib_2015_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arbib_2015-60">[60]</a></sup> and the impact of cognitive constraints and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">biases</a> on human language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In cognitive linguistics, language is approached via the <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">senses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibarretxe-Antuñano_2002_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ibarretxe-Antuñano_2002-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs&amp;Colston_1995_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbs&amp;Colston_1995-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>A closely related approach is <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_linguistics" title="Evolutionary linguistics">evolutionary linguistics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> which includes the study of linguistic units as <a href="/wiki/Memetics" title="Memetics">cultural replicators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> It is possible to study how language <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">replicates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapts</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individual</a> or the speech community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Construction_grammar" title="Construction grammar">Construction grammar</a> is a framework which applies the <a href="/wiki/Meme" title="Meme">meme</a> concept to the study of syntax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dahl_2001_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dahl_2001-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirby_2013_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirby_2013-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zehentner_2019_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zehentner_2019-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacWhinney_2015_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacWhinney_2015-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The generative versus evolutionary approach are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Structuralism_(biology)" title="Structuralism (biology)">formalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptationism" title="Adaptationism">functionalism</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This reference is however different from the use of the terms in <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">human sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodology">Methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Linguistics is primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descriptive_linguistics" title="Descriptive linguistics">descriptive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martinet_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martinet-75">[75]</a></sup> Linguists describe and explain features of language without making subjective judgments on whether a particular feature or usage is "good" or "bad". This is analogous to practice in other sciences: a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoologist" title="Zoologist">zoologist</a> studies the animal kingdom without making subjective judgments on whether a particular species is "better" or "worse" than another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Linguistic_prescription" title="Linguistic prescription">Prescription</a>, on the other hand, is an attempt to promote particular linguistic usages over others, often favouring a particular dialect or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acrolect" title="Acrolect">acrolect</a>". This may have the aim of establishing a <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">linguistic standard</a>, which can aid communication over large geographical areas. It may also, however, be an attempt by speakers of one language or dialect to exert influence over speakers of other languages or dialects (see <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_imperialism" title="Linguistic imperialism">Linguistic imperialism</a>). An extreme version of prescriptivism can be found among <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">censors</a>, who attempt to eradicate words and structures that they consider to be destructive to society. Prescription, however, may be practised appropriately in <a href="/wiki/Language_education" title="Language education">language instruction</a>, like in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_language_teaching" title="English language teaching">ELT</a>, where certain fundamental grammatical rules and lexical items need to be introduced to a second-language speaker who is attempting to <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">acquire</a> the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most contemporary linguists work under the assumption that <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken data</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sign_language" title="Sign language">signed data</a> are more fundamental than <a href="/wiki/Written_language" title="Written language">written data</a>. This is because
</p>
<ul><li>Speech appears to be universal to all human beings capable of producing and perceiving it, while there have been many <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a> and speech communities that lack written communication;</li>
<li>Features appear in speech which aren't always recorded in writing, including <a href="/wiki/Phonological_rule" title="Phonological rule">phonological rules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">sound changes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speech_error" title="Speech error">speech errors</a>;</li>
<li>All natural writing systems reflect a spoken language (or potentially a signed one), even with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pictographic" title="Pictographic">pictographic</a> scripts like <a href="/wiki/Dongba" title="Dongba">Dongba</a> writing <a href="/wiki/Naxi_language" title="Naxi language">Naxi</a> <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a> with the same pictogram, and text in writing systems used for <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_writing_system" title="List of languages by writing system">two languages</a> changing to fit the spoken language being recorded;</li>
<li>Speech evolved before human beings invented writing;</li>
<li>Individuals learn to speak and process spoken language more easily and earlier than they do with <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Nonetheless, linguists agree that the study of written language can be worthwhile and valuable. For research that relies on <a href="/wiki/Corpus_linguistics" title="Corpus linguistics">corpus linguistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_linguistics" title="Computational linguistics">computational linguistics</a>, written language is often much more convenient for processing large amounts of linguistic data. Large corpora of spoken language are difficult to create and hard to find, and are typically <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(linguistics)" title="Transcription (linguistics)">transcribed</a> and written. In addition, linguists have turned to text-based discourse occurring in various formats of <a href="/wiki/Computer-mediated_communication" title="Computer-mediated communication">computer-mediated communication</a> as a viable site for linguistic inquiry.
</p><p>The study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Writing_systems" title="Writing systems">writing systems</a> themselves, <a href="/wiki/Graphemics" title="Graphemics">graphemics</a>, is, in any case, considered a branch of linguistics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis">Analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the 20th century, linguists analysed language on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diachronic_linguistics" title="Diachronic linguistics">diachronic</a> plane, which was historical in focus. This meant that they would compare linguistic features and try to analyse language from the point of view of how it had changed between then and later. However, with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Saussure" title="Ferdinand de Saussure">Saussurean</a> linguistics in the 20th century, the focus shifted to a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronic_linguistics" title="Synchronic linguistics">synchronic</a> approach, where the study was geared towards analysis and comparison between different language variations, which existed at the same given point of time.
</p><p>At another level, the <a href="/wiki/Syntagmatic_analysis" title="Syntagmatic analysis">syntagmatic</a> plane of linguistic analysis entails the comparison between the way words are sequenced, within the syntax of a sentence. For example, the article "the" is followed by a noun, because of the syntagmatic relation between the words. The <a href="/wiki/Paradigmatic_analysis" title="Paradigmatic analysis">paradigmatic</a> plane on the other hand, focuses on an analysis that is based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paradigms" title="Paradigms">paradigms</a> or concepts that are embedded in a given text. In this case, words of the same type or class may be replaced in the text with each other to achieve the same conceptual understanding.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_linguistics" title="History of linguistics">History of linguistics</a></div>
<p>The earliest activities in the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_description" title="Linguistic description">description of language</a> have been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/6th_century_BC" title="6th century BC">6th-century-BC</a> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> grammarian <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> who wrote a <a href="/wiki/Formal_grammar" title="Formal grammar">formal description</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit language</a> in his <i><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">Aṣṭādhyāyī</i></span></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Today, modern-day theories on <a href="/wiki/Generative_grammar" title="Generative grammar">grammar</a> employ many of the principles that were laid down then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before the 20th century, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a></i>, first attested in 1716,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Etymonline_Definition_of_Philology_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Etymonline_Definition_of_Philology-83">[83]</a></sup> was commonly used to refer to the study of language, which was then predominantly historical in focus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Introduction:_Philology_in_a_Manuscript_Culture_by_Stephen_G._Nichols_(Vol._65,_No._1)_(Jan_1990),_pp._1–10._Published_by_Medieval_Academy_of_America._84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction:_Philology_in_a_Manuscript_Culture_by_Stephen_G._Nichols_(Vol._65,_No._1)_(Jan_1990),_pp._1–10._Published_by_Medieval_Academy_of_America.-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Since <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Saussure" title="Ferdinand de Saussure">Ferdinand de Saussure</a>'s insistence on the importance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronic_analysis_(linguistics)" title="Synchronic analysis (linguistics)">synchronic analysis</a>, however, this focus has shifted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and the term <i>philology</i> is now generally used for the "study of a language's grammar, history, and literary tradition", especially in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> (where philology has never been very popularly considered as the "science of language").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Etymonline_Definition_of_Philology_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Etymonline_Definition_of_Philology-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the term <i>linguist</i> in the sense of "a student of language" dates from 1641,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline_linguist_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline_linguist-88">[88]</a></sup> the term <i>linguistics</i> is first attested in 1847.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline_linguist_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline_linguist-88">[88]</a></sup> It is now the usual term in English for the scientific study of language,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> though <i>linguistic science</i> is sometimes used.
</p><p>Linguistics is a <a href="/wiki/Interdisciplinarity" title="Interdisciplinarity">multi-disciplinary</a> field of research that combines tools from natural sciences, social sciences, <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal sciences</a>, and the humanities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Many linguists, such as David Crystal, conceptualize the field as being primarily scientific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>  The term <i>linguist</i> applies to someone who studies language or is a researcher within the field, or to someone who uses the tools of the discipline to describe and analyse specific languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_Heritage_2000_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-American_Heritage_2000-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_grammarians">Early grammarians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Linguistics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Early grammarians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">Philology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammarian_(Greco-Roman)" title="Grammarian (Greco-Roman)">Grammarian (Greco-Roman)</a></div>
<p>The formal study of language began in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> with <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a>, the 6th century BC grammarian who formulated 3,959 rules of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a> <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>. Pāṇini's systematic classification of the sounds of Sanskrit into <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>, and word classes, such as nouns and verbs, was the first known instance of its kind. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sibawayh" title="Sibawayh">Sibawayh</a>, a Persian, made a detailed description of Arabic in AD 760 in his monumental work, <i>Al-kitab fii an-naħw</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">الكتاب في النحو</span></span>, <i>The Book on Grammar</i>), the first known author to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">sounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonemes (sounds as units of a linguistic system)</a>.  Western interest in the study of languages began somewhat later than in the East,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> but the grammarians of the classical languages did not use the same methods or reach the same conclusions as their contemporaries in the Indic world. Early interest in language in the West was a part of philosophy, not of grammatical description. The first insights into semantic theory were made by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)"><i>Cratylus</i> dialogue</a>, where he argues that words denote concepts that are eternal and exist in the world of ideas. This work is the first to use the word <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> to describe the history of a word's meaning.
